      , provoking accusations of defeatism. He saw the Turks as a
      <a href="/wiki/Scourge" title="Scourge">
       scourge
      </a>
      sent to punish Christians by God, as agents of the Biblical
      <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" title="Apocalypse">
       apocalypse
      </a>
      that would destroy the
      <a href="/wiki/Antichrist" title="Antichrist">
       antichrist
      </a>
      , whom Luther believed to be the papacy, and the Roman Church.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He consistently rejected the idea of a
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_war" title="Religious war">
       Holy War
      </a>
      , "as though our people were an army of Christians against the Turks, who were enemies of Christ. This is absolutely contrary to Christ's doctrine and name".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On the other hand, in keeping with his
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_two_kingdoms" title="Doctrine of the two kingdoms">
       doctrine of the two kingdoms
      </a>
      , Luther did support non-religious war against the Turks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
       <a href="#cite_note-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1526, he argued in
      <i>
       Whether Soldiers can be in a State of Grace
      </i>
      that national defence is reason for a just war.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 1529, in
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/On_War_against_the_Turk" title="On War against the Turk">
        On War against the Turk
       </a>
      </i>
      , he was actively urging Emperor
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">
       Charles V
      </a>
      and the German people to fight a secular war against the Turks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He made clear, however, that the spiritual war against an alien faith was separate, to be waged through prayer and repentance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
       <a href="#cite_note-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Around the time of the Siege of Vienna, Luther wrote a prayer for national deliverance from the Turks, asking God to "give to our emperor perpetual victory over our enemies".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1542, Luther read a Latin translation of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an">
       Qur'an
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
       <a href="#cite_note-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He went on to produce several critical pamphlets on Islam, which he called "Mohammedanism" or "the Turk".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
       <a href="#cite_note-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though Luther saw the Muslim faith as a tool of the devil, he was indifferent to its practice: "Let the Turk believe and live as he will, just as one lets the papacy and other false Christians live."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He opposed banning the publication of the Qur'an, wanting it exposed to scrutiny.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Antinomian_controversy">
       Antinomian controversy
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Early in 1537,
      <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Agricola" title="Johannes Agricola">
       Johannes Agricola
      </a>
      (14941566) serving at the time as pastor in Luther's birthplace, Eisleben preached a sermon in which he claimed that God's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Good_news_(Christianity)" title="Good news (Christianity)">
       gospel
      </a>
      , not God's moral law (the
      <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">
       Ten Commandments
      </a>
      ), revealed God's wrath to Christians. Based on this sermon and others by Agricola, Luther suspected that Agricola was behind certain anonymous
      <a href="/wiki/Antinomianism" title="Antinomianism">
       antinomian
      </a>
      theses circulating in Wittenberg. These theses asserted that the law is no longer to be taught to Christians but belonged only to city hall.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther responded to these theses with six series of theses against Agricola and the antinomians, four of which became the basis for
      <a href="/wiki/Disputation" title="Disputation">
       disputations
      </a>
      between 1538 and 1540.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He also responded to these assertions in other writings, such as his 1539
      <a href="/wiki/Open_letter" title="Open letter">
       open letter
      </a>
      to C. Gttel
      <i>
       Against the Antinomians
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
       <a href="#cite_note-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and his book
      <i>
       On the Councils and the Church
      </i>
      from the same year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
       <a href="#cite_note-188">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        188
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In his theses and disputations against the antinomians, Luther reviews and reaffirms, on the one hand, what has been called the "second use of the law," that is, the law as the Holy Spirit's tool to work sorrow over sin in man's heart, thus preparing him for Christ's fulfillment of the law offered in the gospel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
       <a href="#cite_note-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther states that everything that is used to work sorrow over sin is called the law, even if it is Christ's life, Christ's death for sin, or God's goodness experienced in creation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Simply refusing to preach the Ten Commandments among Christians thereby, as it were, removing the three letters l-a-w from the church does not eliminate the accusing law.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
       <a href="#cite_note-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Claiming that the law in any form should not be preached to Christians anymore would be tantamount to asserting that Christians are no longer sinners in themselves and that the church consists only of essentially holy people.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
       <a href="#cite_note-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On the other hand, Luther also points out that the Ten Commandments when considered not as God's condemning judgment but as an expression of his eternal will, that is, of the natural law also positively teach how the Christian ought to live.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
       <a href="#cite_note-193">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        193
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This has traditionally been called the "third use of the law."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
       <a href="#cite_note-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For Luther, also Christ's life, when understood as an example, is nothing more than an illustration of the Ten Commandments, which a Christian should follow in his or her
      <a href="/wiki/Vocation" title="Vocation">
       vocations
      </a>
      on a daily basis.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
       <a href="#cite_note-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The Ten Commandments, and the beginnings of the renewed life of Christians accorded to them by the sacrament of
      <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism#Baptism" title="Lutheranism">
       baptism
      </a>
      , are a present foreshadowing of the believers' future
      <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">
       angel
      </a>
      -like life in heaven in the midst of this life.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
       <a href="#cite_note-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther's teaching of the Ten Commandments, therefore, has clear
      <a href="/wiki/Eschatology" title="Eschatology">
       eschatological
      </a>
      overtones, which, characteristically for Luther, do not encourage world-flight but direct the Christian to service to the neighbor in the common, daily vocations of this perishing world.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Bigamy_of_Philip_of_Hesse">
       Bigamy of Philip of Hesse
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      From December 1539, Luther became implicated in the
      <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">
       bigamy
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Philip_I,_Landgrave_of_Hesse" title="Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse">
       Philip I, Landgrave of Hesse
      </a>
      , who wanted to marry one of his wife's ladies-in-waiting. Philip solicited the approval of Luther, Melanchthon, and Bucer, citing as a precedent the polygamy of the patriarchs. The theologians were not prepared to make a general ruling, and they reluctantly advised the
      <a href="/wiki/Landgrave" title="Landgrave">
       landgrave
      </a>
      that if he was determined, he should marry secretly and keep quiet about the matter.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
       <a href="#cite_note-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As a result, on 4 March 1540, Philip married a second wife, Margarethe von der Sale, with Melanchthon and Bucer among the witnesses. However, Philip was unable to keep the marriage secret, and he threatened to make Luther's advice public. Luther told him to "tell a good, strong lie" and deny the marriage completely, which Philip did during the subsequent public controversy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
       <a href="#cite_note-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the view of Luther's biographer
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Brecht" title="Martin Brecht">
       Martin Brecht
      </a>
      , "giving confessional advice for Philip of Hesse was one of the worst mistakes Luther made, and, next to the landgrave himself, who was directly responsible for it, history chiefly holds Luther accountable".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
       <a href="#cite_note-199">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        199
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Brecht argues that Luther's mistake was not that he gave private pastoral advice, but that he miscalculated the political implications.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
       <a href="#cite_note-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The affair caused lasting damage to Luther's reputation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
       <a href="#cite_note-201">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        201
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-Judaism_and_antisemitism">
       Anti-Judaism and antisemitism
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_and_antisemitism" title="Martin Luther and antisemitism">
       Martin Luther and antisemitism
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_antisemitism" title="Christianity and antisemitism">
       Christianity and antisemitism
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="340" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg/170px-1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg/255px-1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/49/1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1543_On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies_by_Martin_Luther.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The original title page of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies" title="On the Jews and Their Lies">
          On the Jews and Their Lies
         </a>
        </i>
        , written by Martin Luther in 1543
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Luther wrote about the Jews throughout his career, though only a few of his works dealt with them directly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
       <a href="#cite_note-202">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        202
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther rarely encountered Jews during his life, but his attitudes reflected a theological and cultural tradition which saw Jews as a rejected people guilty of the murder of Christ, and he lived within a local community that had expelled Jews some ninety years earlier.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
       <a href="#cite_note-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He considered the Jews blasphemers and liars because they rejected the divinity of Jesus, whereas Christians believed Jesus was the
      <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">
       Messiah
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
       <a href="#cite_note-204">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        204
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      But Luther believed that all human beings who set themselves against God were equally guilty.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
       <a href="#cite_note-205">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        205
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As early as 1516, he wrote that many people "are proud with marvelous stupidity when they call the Jews dogs, evildoers, or whatever they like, while they too, and equally, do not realize who or what they are in the sight of God".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
       <a href="#cite_note-206">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        206
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1523, Luther advised kindness toward the Jews in
      <i>
       That Jesus Christ was Born a Jew
      </i>
      and also aimed to convert them to Christianity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
       <a href="#cite_note-207">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        207
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When his efforts at conversion failed, he grew increasingly bitter toward them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
       <a href="#cite_note-208">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        208
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In his 2010 book
      <i>
       Bonhoeffer: Pastor, Martyr, Prophet, Spy
      </i>
      , Christian author
      <a href="/wiki/Eric_Metaxas" title="Eric Metaxas">
       Eric Metaxas
      </a>
      claimed that Luther's attitude towards Jews "unraveled along with his health."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209">
       <a href="#cite_note-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        209
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Luther's other major works on the Jews were his 60,000-word treatise
      <i>
       Von den Juden und Ihren Lgen
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/On_the_Jews_and_Their_Lies" title="On the Jews and Their Lies">
        On the Jews and Their Lies
       </a>
      </i>
      ), and
      <i>
       Vom Schem Hamphoras und vom Geschlecht Christi
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       On the Holy Name and the Lineage of Christ
      </i>
      ), both published in 1543, three years before his death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210">
       <a href="#cite_note-210">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        210
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther argued that the Jews were no longer the chosen people but "the devil's people", and referred to them with violent, vile language.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
       <a href="#cite_note-211">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        211
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212">
       <a href="#cite_note-212">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        212
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Citing Deuteronomy 13, wherein
      <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">
       Moses
      </a>
      commands the killing of idolaters and the burning of their cities and property as an offering to God, Luther called for a "
      <i>
       scharfe Barmherzigkeit
      </i>
      " ("sharp mercy") against the Jews "to see whether we might save at least a few from the glowing flames."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gritsch8687_213-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gritsch8687-213">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        213
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther advocated setting synagogues on fire, destroying Jewish
      <a href="/wiki/Siddur" title="Siddur">
       prayerbooks
      </a>
      , forbidding rabbis from preaching, seizing Jews' property and money, and smashing up their homes, so that these "envenomed worms" would be forced into labour or expelled "for all time".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214">
       <a href="#cite_note-214">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Robert Michael's view, Luther's words "We are at fault in not slaying them" amounted to a sanction for murder.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
       <a href="#cite_note-215">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        215
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "God's anger with them is so intense," Luther concluded, "that gentle mercy will only tend to make them worse, while sharp mercy will reform them but little. Therefore, in any case, away with them!"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gritsch8687_213-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gritsch8687-213">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        213
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Luther spoke out against the Jews in Saxony, Brandenburg, and Silesia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg_216-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg-216">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        216
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Josel_of_Rosheim" title="Josel of Rosheim">
       Josel of Rosheim
      </a>
      , the Jewish spokesman who tried to help the Jews of Saxony in 1537, later blamed their plight on "that priest whose name was Martin Luthermay his body and soul be bound up in hell!who wrote and issued many heretical books in which he said that whoever would help the Jews was doomed to perdition."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
       <a href="#cite_note-217">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        217
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Josel asked the city of Strasbourg to forbid the sale of Luther's anti-Jewish works: they refused initially, but did so when a Lutheran pastor in
      <a href="/wiki/Hochfelden,_Bas-Rhin" title="Hochfelden, Bas-Rhin">
       Hochfelden
      </a>
      used a sermon to urge his parishioners to murder Jews.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg_216-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Michael:Josel.2FStrasbourg-216">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        216
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther's influence persisted after his death. Throughout the 1580s, riots led to the expulsion of Jews from several German Lutheran states.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
       <a href="#cite_note-218">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        218
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Luther was the most widely read author of his generation, and within Germany he acquired the status of a prophet.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
       <a href="#cite_note-219">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        219
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to the prevailing view among historians,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wallman1_220-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wallman1-220">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        220
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      his anti-Jewish rhetoric contributed significantly to the development of antisemitism in Germany,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221">
       <a href="#cite_note-221">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        221
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and in the 1930s and 1940s provided an "ideal underpinning" for the Nazis' attacks on Jews.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222">
       <a href="#cite_note-222">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Reinhold Lewin writes that anybody who "wrote against the Jews for whatever reason believed he had the right to justify himself by triumphantly referring to Luther." According to Michael, just about every anti-Jewish book printed in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Reich" title="Third Reich">
       Third Reich
      </a>
      contained references to and quotations from Luther.
      <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Himmler" title="Heinrich Himmler">
       Heinrich Himmler
      </a>
      wrote admiringly of his writings and sermons on the Jews in 1940.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223">
       <a href="#cite_note-223">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        223
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The city of Nuremberg presented a first edition of
      <i>
       On the Jews and their Lies
      </i>
      to
      <a href="/wiki/Julius_Streicher" title="Julius Streicher">
       Julius Streicher
      </a>
      , editor of the Nazi newspaper
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Der_St%C3%BCrmer" title="Der Strmer">
        Der Strmer
       </a>
      </i>
      , on his birthday in 1937; the newspaper described it as the most radically anti-Semitic tract ever published.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
       <a href="#cite_note-224">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        224
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was publicly exhibited in a glass case at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuremberg_rallies" title="Nuremberg rallies">
       Nuremberg rallies
      </a>
      and quoted in a 54-page explanation of the Aryan Law by Dr. E.H. Schulz and Dr. R. Frercks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
       <a href="#cite_note-225">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        225
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On 17 December 1941, seven Protestant regional church confederations issued a statement agreeing with the policy of forcing Jews to wear the
      <a href="/wiki/Yellow_badge" title="Yellow badge">
       yellow badge
      </a>
      , "since after his bitter experience Luther had already suggested preventive measures against the Jews and their expulsion from German territory." According to
      <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Goldhagen" title="Daniel Goldhagen">
       Daniel Goldhagen
      </a>
      , Bishop Martin Sasse, a leading Protestant churchman, published a compendium of Luther's writings shortly after
      <a href="/wiki/Kristallnacht" title="Kristallnacht">
       Kristallnacht
      </a>
      , for which
      <a href="/wiki/Diarmaid_MacCulloch" title="Diarmaid MacCulloch">
       Diarmaid MacCulloch
      </a>
      , Professor of the History of the Church in the University of Oxford argued that Luther's writing was a "blueprint."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226">
       <a href="#cite_note-226">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        226
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Sasse applauded the burning of the synagogues and the coincidence of the day, writing in the introduction, "On 10 November 1938, on Luther's birthday, the synagogues are burning in Germany." The German people, he urged, ought to heed these words "of the greatest antisemite of his time, the warner of his people against the Jews."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227">
       <a href="#cite_note-227">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        227
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="560" data-file-width="600" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg/170px-Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg/255px-Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg/340px-Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Judensau-Wittenberg.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Judensau" title="Judensau">
         Judensau
        </a>
        on the Wittenberg Church, built 13001470.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      At the heart of scholars' debate about Luther's influence is whether it is
      <a href="/wiki/Anachronism" title="Anachronism">
       anachronistic
      </a>
      to view his work as a precursor of the racial antisemitism of the Nazis. Some scholars see Luther's influence as limited, and the Nazis' use of his work as opportunistic. Biographer
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Brecht" title="Martin Brecht">
       Martin Brecht
      </a>
      points out that "There is a world of difference between his belief in salvation and a racial ideology. Nevertheless, his misguided agitation had the evil result that Luther fatefully became one of the 'church fathers' of anti-Semitism and thus provided material for the modern hatred of the Jews, cloaking it with the authority of the Reformer."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228">
       <a href="#cite_note-228">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        228
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Johannes Wallmann argues that Luther's writings against the Jews were largely ignored in the 18th and 19th centuries, and that there was no continuity between Luther's thought and Nazi ideology.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229">
       <a href="#cite_note-229">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        229
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Uwe_Siemon-Netto" title="Uwe Siemon-Netto">
       Uwe Siemon-Netto
      </a>
      agreed, arguing that it was because the Nazis were already anti-Semites that they revived Luther's work.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230">
       <a href="#cite_note-230">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        230
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SiemonNetto2_231-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SiemonNetto2-231">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        231
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hans J. Hillerbrand agreed that to focus on Luther was to adopt an essentially ahistorical perspective of Nazi antisemitism that ignored other contributory factors in
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Germany" title="History of Germany">
       German history
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HillerbrandEB_232-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HillerbrandEB-232">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        232
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Similarly,
      <a href="/wiki/Roland_Bainton" title="Roland Bainton">
       Roland Bainton
      </a>
      , noted church historian and Luther biographer, wrote "One could wish that Luther had died before ever [
      <i>
       On the Jews and Their Lies
      </i>
      ] was written. His position was entirely religious and in no respect racial."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233">
       <a href="#cite_note-233">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        233
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234">
       <a href="#cite_note-234">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        234
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other scholars argue that, even if his views were merely
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Judaism" title="Anti-Judaism">
       anti-Judaic
      </a>
      that is, opposed to Judaism and its adherence rather than the Jews as an ethnic grouptheir violence lent a new element to the standard Christian suspicion of Judaism. Ronald Berger writes that Luther is credited with "Germanizing the Christian critique of Judaism and establishing anti-Semitism as a key element of German culture and national identity."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger28_235-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berger28-235">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        235
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lawrence_Rose" title="Paul Lawrence Rose">
       Paul Rose
      </a>
      argues that he caused a "hysterical and demonizing mentality" about Jews to enter German thought and discourse, a mentality that might otherwise have been absent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236">
       <a href="#cite_note-236">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        236
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some scholars, such as Mark U. Edwards in his book
      <i>
       Luther's Last Battles: Politics and Polemics 153146
      </i>
      (1983), suggest that since Luther's increasingly antisemitic views developed during the years his health deteriorated, it is possible they were at least partly the product of a declining state of mind. Edwards also comments that Luther often deliberately used "vulgarity and violence" for effect, both in his writings condemning the Jews and in diatribes against "Turks" (Muslims) and Catholics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237">
       <a href="#cite_note-237">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        237
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the 1980s, Lutheran Church denominations have repudiated Martin Luther's statements against the Jews and have rejected the use of them to incite hatred against Lutherans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238">
       <a href="#cite_note-238">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        238
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239">
       <a href="#cite_note-239">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        239
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Strommen et al.'s 1970 survey of 4,745 North American Lutherans aged 1565 found that, compared to the other minority groups under consideration, Lutherans were the least prejudiced toward Jews.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240">
       <a href="#cite_note-240">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        240
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Depiction_of_Luther">
       Depiction of Luther
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      In the 1530s and 1540s, printed images of Luther that emphasized his monumental size were crucial to the spread of Protestantism. In contrast to images of frail Catholic saints, Luther was presented as a stout man with a "double chin, strong mouth, piercing deep-set eyes, fleshy face, and squat neck." He was shown to be physically imposing, an equal in stature to the secular German princes with whom he would join forces to spread Lutheranism. His large body also let the viewer know that he did not shun earthly pleasures like drinkingbehavior that was a stark contrast to the ascetic life of the medieval religious orders. Famous images from this period include the woodcuts by
      <a href="/wiki/Hans_Brosamer" title="Hans Brosamer">
       Hans Brosamer
      </a>
      (1530) and
      <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">
       Lucas Cranach the Elder
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Younger" title="Lucas Cranach the Younger">
       Lucas Cranach the Younger
      </a>
      (1546).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241">
       <a href="#cite_note-241">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        241
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years_and_death">
       Final years and death
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1632" data-file-width="1232" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg/170px-Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg/255px-Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg/340px-Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luthers_Sterbehaus_Eisleben.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther%27s_Death_House" title="Martin Luther's Death House">
         The house where Luther died
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2216" data-file-width="1616" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg/220px-DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg/330px-DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg/440px-DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DHM_-_Luther_auf_Totenbett.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Luther on his deathbed by
        <a href="/wiki/Lucas_Cranach_the_Elder" title="Lucas Cranach the Elder">
         Lucas Cranach the Elder
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Luther had been suffering from ill health for years, including
      <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9ni%C3%A8re%27s_disease" title="Mnire's disease">
       Mnire's disease
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertigo_(medical)" title="Vertigo (medical)">
       vertigo
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fainting" title="Fainting">
       fainting
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Tinnitus" title="Tinnitus">
       tinnitus
      </a>
      , and a
      <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">
       cataract
      </a>
      in one eye.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9019884_242-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pmid9019884-242">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        242
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From 1531 to 1546, his health deteriorated further. The years of struggle with Rome, the antagonisms with and among his fellow reformers, and the scandal which ensued from the
      <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">
       bigamy
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_of_Hesse" title="Philip of Hesse">
       Philip of Hesse
      </a>
      incident, in which Luther had played a leading role, all may have contributed. In 1536, he began to suffer from
      <a href="/wiki/Kidney_stone" title="Kidney stone">
       kidney and bladder stones
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">
       arthritis
      </a>
      , and an ear infection ruptured an ear drum. In December 1544, he began to feel the effects of
      <a href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" title="Angina pectoris">
       angina
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243">
       <a href="#cite_note-243">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        243
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      His poor physical health made him short-tempered and even harsher in his writings and comments. His wife Katharina was overheard saying, "Dear husband, you are too rude," and he responded, "They are teaching me to be rude."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244">
       <a href="#cite_note-244">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        244
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1545 and 1546 Luther preached three times in the
      <a href="/wiki/Marktkirche_Unser_Lieben_Frauen" title="Marktkirche Unser Lieben Frauen">
       Market Church
      </a>
      in Halle, staying with his friend
      <a href="/wiki/Justus_Jonas" title="Justus Jonas">
       Justus Jonas
      </a>
      during
      <a href="/wiki/Christmas" title="Christmas">
       Christmas
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245">
       <a href="#cite_note-245">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        245
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg/170px-Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg/255px-Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg/340px-Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luthertombstoneunderaltar.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Luther's tombstone in the Castle Church in Wittenberg.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      His last sermon was delivered at
      <a href="/wiki/Eisleben" title="Eisleben">
       Eisleben
      </a>
      , his place of birth, on 15 February 1546, three days before his death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246">
       <a href="#cite_note-246">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        246
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It was "entirely devoted to the obdurate Jews, whom it was a matter of great urgency to expel from all German territory," according to
      <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Poliakov" title="Lon Poliakov">
       Lon Poliakov
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247">
       <a href="#cite_note-247">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        247
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      James Mackinnon writes that it concluded with a "fiery summons to drive the Jews bag and baggage from their midst, unless they desisted from their calumny and their usury and became Christians."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248">
       <a href="#cite_note-248">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        248
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Luther said, "we want to practice Christian love toward them and pray that they convert," but also that they are "our public enemies... and if they could kill us all, they would gladly do so. And so often they do."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249">
       <a href="#cite_note-249">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        249
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Luther's final journey, to Mansfeld, was taken because of his concern for his siblings' families continuing in their father Hans Luther's copper mining trade. Their livelihood was threatened by Count Albrecht of Mansfeld bringing the industry under his own control. The controversy that ensued involved all four Mansfeld counts: Albrecht, Philip, John George, and Gerhard. Luther journeyed to Mansfeld twice in late 1545 to participate in the negotiations for a settlement, and a third visit was needed in early 1546 for their completion.
     </p>
     <p>
      The negotiations were successfully concluded on 17 February 1546. After 8:00pm, he experienced chest pains. When he went to his bed, he prayed, "Into your hand I commit my spirit; you have redeemed me, O Lord, faithful God" (Ps. 31:5), the common prayer of the dying. At 1:00am he awoke with more chest pain and was warmed with hot towels. He thanked God for revealing his Son to him in whom he had believed. His companions, Justus Jonas and Michael Coelius, shouted loudly, "Reverend father, are you ready to die trusting in your Lord Jesus Christ and to confess the doctrine which you have taught in his name?" A distinct "Yes" was Luther's reply.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2429" data-file-width="2333" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg/170px-Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg/255px-Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a3/Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg/340px-Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Luther_death-hand_mask.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Cast of Luther's face and hands at his death, in the Market Church in Halle
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250">
         <a href="#cite_note-250">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          250
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      An apoplectic stroke deprived him of his speech, and he died shortly afterwards at 2:45a.m. on 18 February 1546, aged 62, in Eisleben, the city of his birth. He was buried in the Castle Church in
      <a href="/wiki/Wittenberg" title="Wittenberg">
       Wittenberg
      </a>
      , beneath the pulpit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251">
       <a href="#cite_note-251">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        251
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The funeral was held by his friends
      <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Bugenhagen" title="Johannes Bugenhagen">
       Johannes Bugenhagen
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Philipp_Melanchthon" title="Philipp Melanchthon">
       Philipp Melanchthon
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacKim19_252-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MacKim19-252">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        252
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A year later, troops of Luther's adversary
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">
       Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor
      </a>
      entered the town, but were ordered by Charles not to disturb the grave.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacKim19_252-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MacKim19-252">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        252
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A piece of paper was later found on which Luther had written his last statement. The statement was in Latin, apart from "We are beggars," which was in German.
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       1. No one can understand Virgil's Bucolics unless he has been a shepherd for five years. No one can understand Virgil's Georgics, unless he has been a farmer for five years.
      </p>
      <p>
       2. No one can understand Cicero's Letters (or so I teach), unless he has busied himself in the affairs of some prominent state for twenty years.
      </p>
      <p>
       3. Know that no one can have indulged in the Holy Writers sufficiently, unless he has governed churches for a hundred years with the prophets, such as Elijah and Elisha, John the Baptist, Christ and the apostles. Do not assail this divine Aeneid; nay, rather prostrate revere the ground that it treads. We are beggars: this is true.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253">
        <a href="#cite_note-253">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         253
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254">
        <a href="#cite_note-254">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         254
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy_and_commemoration">
       Legacy and commemoration
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="443" data-file-width="863" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg/220px-Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg/330px-Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg/440px-Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Countries_by_percentage_of_Protestants.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Luther's legacy: Protestantism by country in 2010.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Luther is honoured on 18 February with a commemoration in the
      <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_Saints_(Lutheran)" title="Calendar of Saints (Lutheran)">
       Lutheran Calendar of Saints
      </a>
      and in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Episcopal_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America)" title="Calendar of saints (Episcopal Church in the United States of America)">
       Episcopal (United States) Calendar of Saints
      </a>
      . In the
      <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">
       Church of England's Calendar of Saints
      </a>
      he is commemorated on 31 October.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Works_and_editions">
       Works and editions
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1116" data-file-width="1410" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg/220px-LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg/330px-LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg/440px-LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LutherWritings%26Portrait1581.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A 1581 German edition of Luther's "Tabletalks"
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The Erlangen Edition (
       <i>
        Erlangener Ausgabe
       </i>
       : "EA"), comprising the
       <i>
        Exegetica opera latina
       </i>
        Latin exegetical works of Luther.
      </li>
      <li>
       The Weimar Edition (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weimarer_Ausgabe" title="Weimarer Ausgabe">
        Weimarer Ausgabe
       </a>
       ) is the exhaustive, standard German edition of Luther's Latin and German works, indicated by the abbreviation "WA". This is continued into "WA Br"
       <i>
        Weimarer Ausgabe, Briefwechsel
       </i>
       (correspondence), "WA Tr"
       <i>
        Weimarer Ausgabe, Tischreden
       </i>
       (tabletalk) and "WA DB"
       <i>
        Weimarer Ausgabe, Deutsche Bibel
       </i>
       (German Bible).
      </li>
      <li>
       The American Edition (
       <i>
        Luther's Works
       </i>
       ) is the most extensive English translation of Luther's writings, indicated either by the abbreviation "LW" or "AE". The first 55 volumes were published 1955-1986, and a twenty volume extension (vols. 56-75) is planned of which volumes 58, 60, and 68 have appeared thus far.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>

           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Hendrix, Scott H.
         <a class="external text" href="http://wordandworld.luthersem.edu/content/pdfs/3-4_Luther/3-4_Hendrix.pdf" rel="nofollow">
          "The Controversial Luther"
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          Word &amp; World
         </i>
         3/4 (1983), Luther Seminary, St. Paul, MN, p. 393: "And, finally, after the Holocaust and the use of his anti-Jewish statements by National Socialists, Luther's anti-semitic outbursts are now unmentionable, though they were already repulsive in the sixteenth century. As a result, Luther has become as controversial in the twentieth century as he was in the sixteenth." Also see Hillerbrand, Hans.
         <a class="external text" href="http://cco.cambridge.org/extract?id=ccol0521816483_CCOL0521816483A018" rel="nofollow">
          "The legacy of Martin Luther"
         </a>
         , in Hillerbrand, Hans &amp; McKim, Donald K. (eds.)
         <i>
          The Cambridge Companion to Luther
         </i>
         . Cambridge University Press, 2003. In 1523, Luther wrote that Jesus Christ was born a Jew which discouraged mistreatment of the Jews and advocated their conversion by proving that the
         <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
          Old Testament
         </a>
         could be shown to speak of
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" title="Jesus Christ">
          Jesus Christ
         </a>
         . However, as the Reformation grew, Luther began to lose hope in large-scale Jewish conversion to Christianity, and in the years his health deterioriated he grew more acerbic toward the Jews, writing against them with the kind of venom he had already unleashed on the
         <a href="/wiki/Anabaptists" title="Anabaptists">
          Anabaptists
         </a>
         ,
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zwingli" title="Zwingli">
          Zwingli
         </a>
         , and the
         <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">
          pope
         </a>
         . His
         <i>
          Von den Jden und iren Lgen
         </i>
         in modern German spelling
         <i>
          Von den Juden und ihren Lgen
         </i>
         (
         <i>
          On the Jews and Their Lies
         </i>
         )thus took its place among other anti-Jewish treatises already identified with Rome and Constantinople.
        </span>
       </li>

        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-Brecht12_31-0">
           ^

       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-32">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-32">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         "
         <a href="/wiki/Johann_Tetzel" title="Johann Tetzel">
          Johann Tetzel
         </a>
         ,"
         <i>
          Encyclopdia Britannica
         </i>
         , 2007: "Tetzel's experiences as a preacher of indulgences, especially between 1503 and 1510, led to his appointment as general commissioner by
         <a href="/wiki/Albert_of_Mainz" title="Albert of Mainz">
          Albert, archbishop of Mainz
         </a>
         , who, deeply in debt to pay for a large accumulation of benefices, had to contribute a considerable sum toward the rebuilding of St. Peter's Basilica in Rome. Albrecht obtained permission from
         <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_X" title="Pope Leo X">
          Pope Leo X
         </a>
         to conduct the sale of a special plenary indulgence (i.e., remission of the temporal punishment of sin), half of the proceeds of which Albrecht was to claim to pay the fees of his benefices. In effect, Tetzel became a salesman whose product was to cause a scandal in Germany that evolved into the greatest crisis (the Reformation) in the history of the Western church."
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-33">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-33">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         (Trent, l. c., can. xii: "Si quis dixerit, fidem justificantem nihil aliud esse quam fiduciam divinae misericordiae, peccata remittentis propter Christum, vel eam fiduciam solam esse, qua justificamur, a.s.")
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-34">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-34">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         (cf. Trent, Sess. VI, cap. iv, xiv)
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-HillerbrandIndulgences-35">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-HillerbrandIndulgences_35-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-HillerbrandIndulgences_35-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Hillerbrand, Hans J. "Martin Luther: Indulgences and salvation,"
         <i>
          Encyclopdia Britannica
         </i>
         , 2007.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-36">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-36">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Thesis 55 of Tetzel's
         <i>
          One Hundred and Six Theses
         </i>
         . These "Anti-theses" were a reply to Luther's
         <i>
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ninety-Five_Theses" title="Ninety-Five Theses">
           Ninety-Five Theses
          </a>
         </i>
         and were drawn up by Tetzel's friend and former Professor,
         <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Wimpina" title="Konrad Wimpina">
          Konrad Wimpina
         </a>
         . Theses 55 &amp; 56 (responding to Luther's 27th Theses) read: "For a soul to fly out, is for it to obtain the vision of God, which can be
         <i>
          hindered by no interruption
         </i>
         , therefore he errs who says that the soul
         <i>
          cannot fly out
         </i>
         before the coin can jingle in the bottom of the chest." In,
         <i>
          The reformation in Germany
         </i>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Henry_Clay_Vedder" title="Henry Clay Vedder">
          Henry Clay Vedder
         </a>
         , 1914, Macmillon Company, p. 405.
         <a class="external autonumber" href="http://books.google.com/books?id=JQ4QAAAAIAAJ&amp;pg=PA405" rel="nofollow">
          [1]
         </a>
         <i>
          Animam purgatam evolare, est eam visione dei potiri, quod nulla potest intercapedine impediri. Quisquis ergo dicit, non citius posse animam volare, quam in fundo cistae denarius possit tinnire, errat
         </i>
         . In:
         <i>
          D. Martini Lutheri, Opera Latina: Varii Argumenti
         </i>
         , 1865, Henricus Schmidt, ed., Heyder and Zimmer,
         <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">
          Frankfurt am Main
         </a>
         &amp; Erlangen, vol. 1, p. 300. (
         <a href="/wiki/Print_on_demand" title="Print on demand">
          Print on demand
         </a>
         edition:
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabu_Press" title="Nabu Press">
          Nabu Press
         </a>
         , 2010,
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1142405516">
          ISBN 1-142-40551-6
         </a>
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781142405519">
          ISBN 978-1-142-40551-9
         </a>
         ).
         <a class="external autonumber" href="http://books.google.com/books?id=s633jfx_uEUC&amp;pg=PA300" rel="nofollow">
          [2]
         </a>
         See also:
         <img alt="Wikisource-logo.svg" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="410" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/12px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/18px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/24px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" width="12"/>

          "
          <a class="extiw" href="//en.wikisource.org/wiki/Catholic_Encyclopedia_(1913)/Johann_Tetzel" title="wikisource:Catholic Encyclopedia (1913)/Johann Tetzel">

       <li id="cite_note-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005-142">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005_142-0">
          <sup>

             a

          <sup>

            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Christopher_Boyd_Brown_2005_142-2">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             c
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Christopher Boyd Brown,
         <i>
          Singing the Gospel: Lutheran Hymns and the Success of the Reformation.
         </i>
         (2005)

       <li id="cite_note-143">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-143">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.studia-instrumentorum.de/MUSEUM/zistern.htm" rel="nofollow">
           "Waldzither - Bibliography of the 19th century"
          </a>
          . Studia Instrumentorum
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            March 23,
           </span>
           2014
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           Es ist eine unbedingte Notwendigkeit, dass der Deutsche zu seinen Liedern auch ein echt deutsches Begleitinstrument besitzt. Wie der Spanier seine Gitarre (flschlich Laute genannt), der Italiener seine Mandoline, der Englnder das Banjo, der Russe die Balalaika usw. sein Nationalinstrument nennt, so sollte der Deutsche seine Laute, die Waldzither, welche schon von Dr. Martin Luther auf der Wartburg im Thringer Walde (daher der Name Waldzither) gepflegt wurde, zu seinem Nationalinstrument machen. - Liederheft von C. H. Bhm (Hamburg, March 1919)
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMartin+Luther&amp;rft.btitle=Waldzither+-+Bibliography+of+the+19th+century&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.studia-instrumentorum.de%2FMUSEUM%2Fzistern.htm&amp;rft.pub=Studia+Instrumentorum&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-144">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-144">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation journal">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.hymntime.com/tch/htm/f/l/u/flungtot.htm" rel="nofollow">
           "Flung to the heedless winds"
          </a>

         <i>
          Christian Dogmatics
         </i>
         , 3 vols., (Saint Louis: CPH, 1953), 3:512: "These texts surely make it evident that the departed souls of the believers are in a state of blessed enjoyment of God .... A sleep of the sould which includes enjoyment of God (says Luther) cannot be called a false doctrine."

       <li id="cite_note-155">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-155">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Gerhard
         <i>
          Loci Theologici, Locus de Morte
         </i>
         ,  293 ff. Pieper writes: "Luther speaks more guardedly of the state of the soul between death and resurrection than do Gerhard and the later theologians, who transfer some things to the state between death and resurrection which can be said with certainty only of the state after the resurrection" (
         <i>
          Christian Dogmatics
         </i>
         , 3:512, footnote 21).
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-156">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-156">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Article in the Berlinischer Zeitung 1755 in Complete Works ed. Karl Friedrich Theodor Lachmann 1838 p. 59 "Was die Gegner auf alle diese Stellen antworten werden, ist leicht zu errathen. Sie werden sagen, da Luther mit dem Worte Schlaf gar die Begriffe nicht verbinde, welche Herr R. damit verbindet. Wenn Luther sage, da die Seele IS nach dem Tode schlafe, so denke er nichts mehr dabey, als was alle Leute denken, wenn sie den Tod des Schlafes Bruder nennen. Tode ruhe, leugneten auch die nicht, welche ihr Wachen behaupteten:c. Ueberhaupt ist mit Luthers Ansehen bey der ganzen Streitigkeit nichts zu gewinnen"
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-157">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-157">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Exegetica opera Latina, Volumes 56 Martin Luther, ed. Christopf Stephan Elsperger (Gottlieb) p. 120 "Differunt tamen somnus sive quies hujus vitae et futurae. Homon enim in hac vita defatigatus diurno labore, sub noctem intrat in cubiculum suum tanquam in pace, ut ibi dormiat, et ea nocte fruitur quiete, neque quicquam scit de ullo malo sive incendii, sive caedis. Anima autem non sic dormit, sed vigilat, et patitur visiones loquelas Angelorum et Dei. Ideo somnus in futura vita profundior est quam in hac vita et tamen anima coram Deo vivit. Hac similitudine, quam habeo a somno viventia." (Commentary on Genesis
         <i>
          Enarrationes in Genesin
         </i>
         , 15351545)"
        </span>

         </i>
         ed. and tr. H. Sonntag, Minneapolis: Lutheran Press, 2008, 2327.
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780974852966">
          ISBN 978-0-9748529-6-6
         </a>

       <li id="cite_note-186">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-186">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>

        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-187">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-187">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Cf.
         <i>
          Luther's Works
         </i>
         47:107119. There he writes: "Dear God, should it be unbearable that the holy church confesses itself a sinner, believes in the forgiveness of sins, and asks for remission of sin in the Lord's Prayer? How can one know what sin is without the law and conscience? And how will we learn what Christ is, what he did for us, if we do not know what the law is that he fulfilled for us and what sin is, for which he made satisfaction?" (112113).
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-188">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-188">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Cf.
         <i>
          Luther's Works
         </i>
         41, 113114, 143144, 146147. There he said about the antinomians: "They may be fine Easter preachers, but they are very poor Pentecost preachers, for they do not preach
         <i>
          de sanctificatione et vivificatione Spiritus Sancti
         </i>
         , "about the sanctification by the Holy Spirit," but solely about the redemption of Jesus Christ" (114). "Having rejected and being unable to understand the Ten Commandments, ... they see and yet they let the people go on in their public sins, without any renewal or reformation of their lives" (147).
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-189">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>

           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         "The assertion that Luther's expressions of anti-Jewish sentiment have been of major and persistent influence in the centuries after the Reformation, and that there exists a continuity between Protestant
         <a href="/wiki/Anti-Judaism" title="Anti-Judaism">
          anti-Judaism
         </a>
         and modern racially oriented anti-Semitism, is at present wide-spread in the literature; since the Second World War it has understandably become the prevailing opinion." Johannes Wallmann, "The Reception of Luther's Writings on the Jews from the Reformation to the End of the 19th century",
         <i>
          Lutheran Quarterly
         </i>
         , n.s. 1 (Spring 1987) 1:7297.

         <i>
          Encyclopdia Britannica
         </i>
         , 2007. Hillerbrand writes: "His strident pronouncements against the Jews, especially toward the end of his life, have raised the question of whether Luther significantly encouraged the development of German anti-Semitism. Although many scholars have taken this view, this perspective puts far too much emphasis on Luther and not enough on the larger peculiarities of German history."

       Luther, Martin (1974).
       <i>
        Selected Political Writings
       </i>
       , ed. and with an introd. by J. M. Porter. Philadelphia: Fortress Press.

        ISBN 0-8006-1079-2

       <i>
        Luther's Works
       </i>
       , 55 vols. Eds.

       and

      </li>
      <li>

       <i>
